W2=require('webnode2'),LiveNode=require('w2.livenode');LiveNode.onLivedepOperations([{"content": {"html": "<u>Update 2:</u><b> </b>Wow S&amp;P just downgraded the US. I totally just ate my words, heh.<br /><br /><br /><b><u>Update:</u></b> A lot has changed since my answer from April 2011. It is now July 28, 2011, and as it turns out, I would say that the probability of a US default is just as low now as before, but the probability of a US downgrade is much higher than I originally envisioned. S&amp;P and Moody&#039;s are now saying 50-50.<br /><br />As it <i>also</i> turns out, the impacts of a US downgrade (say from AAA to AA+ or AA) is not as dire as I originally thought. I think in general, market consensus is that the bulk of the impact would be psychological. Of course you will have a flight to quality in the markets and see a sell-off in stocks, etc. But at the same time, there wouldn&#039;t really be a significant amount of forced selling by real-money investors and money market funds. Foreign investors likely will not quickly dump their USD investments. Repo haircuts likely would increase for Treasuries, Agencies, and Agency MBS, but not to debilitating levels. Bank capital requirements under Basel III are not dependent on credit agency ratings so the banks should be okay as well. <br /><br />Over the short term, interest rates would do weird things (buying the short end, selling the long end, curve steepening). Over the long term, risk premium on US Treasuries would likely increase and the cost of borrowing for everyone would increase (since every other interest rate is anchored against the UST rate), but I&#039;m not sure how much that would be. In general, a downgrade would be bad, but not catastrophic. We shall find out soon enough!<br /><br /><br /><b><u>Original Answer:</u></b><u><br /></u>First, I think it is a near impossibility that the US sovereign credit rating will be downgraded from AAA (despite the recent outlook downgrade). But if it <i>does </i>happen, I have some guesses as to what the likely scenarios are.<br /><br />I&#039;ll tackle this using an interest rates framework, and talk about how different markets might react. Basically, two things can happen (forgive me when you see my two answers):<br /><br /><b><u>1. US Treasuries can sell off, a lot</u></b>:<br /><ul><li>This is a no-brainer, a downgrade in US credit will mean that it is less safe. Fewer people will buy these bonds, so the prices go down.</li><li>What this implicitly means is that investors will demand a greater return for their investment, so the interest rate / yield of Treasuries will go up, which necessarily leads to prices declining.</li><li>Investors will likely re-allocate to the new safest alternative assets (if there are even any left). This may include precious metals and commodities (expect those to rally), definitely not equities, corporate credit or other &quot;risky&quot; assets.</li><li>However, there is always the need for passive income for a huge number of investors, so they&#039;re going to be looking for alternative fixed income products to invest in. For instance, German sov debt, Norwegian sov debt, and a few of the other Scandanavian credits that are in theory trading richer than US Treasuries.</li><li>I don&#039;t honestly believe in this first scenario.</li></ul><br /><b><u>2. US Treasuries can rally, a lot:</u></b><br /><ul><li>A US credit downgrade would have a huge impact in the markets and would necessarily imply that some crazy stuff has happened to make this outlandish scenario occur. I.e. the US economy (and through contagion, the world economy) must have deteriorated to such a point that the US is in danger of making timely payments its debt, despite the fact that the US can simply print its own currency.</li><li>&quot;Crazy stuff&quot; is coincides with flight-to-quality (as alluded above), where investors reallocate to safer assets. But it is likely that investors will flee <i>to</i> US Treasuries, rather than away from, because <i>there are no safer assets than US Treasuries. </i>I think in a world in which the US credit rating just got downgraded, it is unlikely that while the US credit deteriorates, other nation&#039;s credits do not simultaneously do the same.</li><li>What this means is that there probably will <i>not</i> be flows into German or Norwegian bonds, or, say, Berkshire Hathaway corporate bonds (which at some point last year traded at a lower yield than Treasuries) because they will likely be similarly downgraded. Credit is all relative, after all.</li><li>And even if these credits<i> do</i> retain their higher ratings, capacity issues would limit how many investors can physically own these bonds. I.e. there is not enough of these bonds outstanding to take the place of the trillions and trillions of US Treasuries that are outstanding and in investors&#039; portfolios.</li><li>There likely will still be a flight to precious metals and other commodities, and equities/corporates will still likely sell off on this news.</li><li>Oh, and to add an empirical example, which may or may not be a valid comparison: When Japan was downgraded in the 1990s, the interest rate on its debt <i>decreased </i>over the next few months/years, which implicitly means that prices have gone <i>up.</i></li></ul><br />So, which of these two scenarios do you think is more likely?<br /><br />These are just some of the impacts on the financial markets that I can think of. Obviously there will be broader impacts in the &quot;real world&quot; too: unemployment, consumer confidence, spending, investments, corporate earnings, international trade, banking and financing, etc. will probably all be negatively affected.", "css": "", "js": "W2.addComponentMetadata({parents: {\"n9P90md\": \"Fe242nf\", \"X8nTW8h\": \"n9P90md\", \"DXhi8kw\": \"n9P90md\", \"b3dX5Tk\": "transition": {"type": "slide"}, "type": "set_node", "domid": "ld_6OpqnQ_13222", "cid": "PtVr59j"}, {"content": {"html": "This is a generalization of Hongwan&#039;s answer. I assume you&#039;re talking about real matrices; there are easy modifications over the complex numbers (a simpler case, actually) and other rings.<br /><br />Your matrix <img src=\"http://d3tf4rqyhjdxvy.cloudfront.net/main-0aab4de5587d853a.png\" width=\"15\" height=\"14\" class=\"math\" title=\"A\" alt=\"A\" /> is similar to a matrix in Jordan form:<br /><br /><img src=\"http://d3tf4rqyhjdxvy.cloudfront.net/main-b0f4cd7a6779a863.png\" width=\"161\" height=\"23\" class=\"math\" title=\"A = P^{-1}(D+U)P\" alt=\"A = P^{-1}(D+U)P\" /><br /><br />where <img src=\"http://d3tf4rqyhjdxvy.cloudfront.net/main-7c12a140515e7e1d.png\" width=\"16\" height=\"14\" class=\"math\" title=\"D\" alt=\"D\" /> is diagonal - the values along its diagonal are the eiganvalues - and <img src=\"http://d3tf4rqyhjdxvy.cloudfront.net/main-f219deb114d59338.png\" width=\"14\" height=\"14\" class=\"math\" title=\"U\" alt=\"U\" /> is all 0&#039;s except perhaps for a few 1&#039;s right above the diagonal. <br /><br />In the generic case, for instance when all the eigenvalues are different, <img src=\"http://d3tf4rqyhjdxvy.cloudfront.net/main-20d2b130caf66bb6.png\" width=\"49\" height=\"14\" class=\"math\" title=\"U=0\" alt=\"U=0\" />. Let&#039;s focus on this case. Now we have:<br /><br /><img src=\"http://d3tf4rqyhjdxvy.cloudfront.net/main-83778dd4b817e77c.png\" width=\"107\" height=\"18\" class=\"math\" title=\"A = P^{-1}DP\" alt=\"A = P^{-1}DP\" />, so<br /><br /><img src=\"http://d3tf4rqyhjdxvy.cloudfront.net/main-a7387a8c17d4ec52.png\" width=\"133\" height=\"18\" class=\"math\" title=\"A^m = P^{-1}D^mP\" alt=\"A^m = P^{-1}D^mP\" /> <br /><br />for any natural number <img src=\"http://d3tf4rqyhjdxvy.cloudfront.net/main-23b52887cf3721b1.png\" width=\"17\" height=\"9\" class=\"math\" title=\"m\" alt=\"m\" />. Thus the powers of <img src=\"http://d3tf4rqyhjdxvy.cloudfront.net/main-0aab4de5587d853a.png\" width=\"15\" height=\"14\" class=\"math\" title=\"A\" alt=\"A\" /> are controlled by powers of the diagonal elements - namely, the eigenvalues:<br /><br /><ul><li>If an eigenvalue has absolute value less than 1, its powers diminish to 0.</li><li>If an eigenvalue has absolute value greater than 1, its powers explode to infinity.</li><li>If an eigenvalue has absolute value exactly 1, its powers also have absolute value exactly 1, so they essentially oscillate (not necessarily repeating themselves, but staying on the unit circle).</li></ul><br />In the non-diagonalizable case, where the Jordan form actually has some 1&#039;s above the diagonal, those 1&#039;s will keep growing (linearly) as you take successive powers, which isn&#039;t what you want. <br /><br />So if you want the powers of a matrix to stay bounded both from above and from below, make sure it is diagonalizable and that all its eigenvalues have absolute value 1.", "css": "", "js": "W2.addComponentMetadata({parents: {\"jVmpeyt\": \"L6jd3Yn\", \"L6jd3Yn\": \"ojBUnQD\", \"fQGPbNk\": \"L6jd3Yn\", A few values, among many, are:<br /><br /><b>It allows our community to build the tools we can&#039;t.</b> We have a fantastic developer community building all sorts of incredible things, including bringing the foursquare experience to platforms we just don&#039;t have the resources to address ourselves. As a small company, we benefit greatly from these campaigns, and there&#039;s no way these rich experiences could happen if every partnership required significant engineering effort on our end.<br /><br /><b>It&#039;s one way of giving back.</b> This kind of POI dataset wasn&#039;t available back when foursquare started, and we&#039;ve been blessed with an extraordinary user community that helped build it. It&#039;s probably one of the most &quot;fluid&quot; roles and depends on the culture of company.<br /><br />An engineering starting point is great (my starting point as well). The transition is from taking responsibility on a code that is supposed to do X into a more holistic view of the product. What&#039;s the vision/road-map/customers<wbr />/tech-needs/mktg/etc?<br /><br />Think about it as the CEO of the product - you are in charge of orchestrating all aspects that are related to the program.  As such, it&#039;s one way to demonstrate that you have the technical and procedural knowledge to manage high-value, long-term projects.<br /><br />Whether this is of value as a product manager really depends on how the organization that you want to work for defines what their product managers are and do on a daily basis.  There are some Product Management roles that are really just mis-labeled Project Management roles.  For these, a PMP certification from the PMI is probably a great &quot;in&quot;.  For other roles, that are less focused on Project Management and more market- or customer-focused, a PMP certification might actually be a detractor in some instances.<br /><br />If you&#039;re really targeting a Product Management role, I would recommend that you look into the professional certifications that exist for that job - Pragmatic Marketing has one, as does Pivotal.", "css": "", "js": personal thoughts on this topic.  Palo Alto and Manila are worlds apart, both literally and figuratively.  Since we don&#039;t have the whole picture regarding your transfer it would be difficult to give a straight answer whether it be yes or no.  I&#039;ll just enumerate some of the pros and cons of living in Manila.<br /><br />Pros: Cost of living is lower (food, rent, household help, etc.), English is the primary method of instruction in schools so most Filipinos speak English, several expats, lots of after-work recreational activities (clubs, gyms, bars, sports centers, malls, etc.), most Filipinos are hospitable and courteous, and if you&#039;d like to take off for the weekend you can drive to some good (not exceptionally great) beaches in just under two hours.  If you want to visit the best beaches in the Philippines you&#039;ll have to take a short plane ride to Palawan, Boracay or Caramoan.  <br /><br />Cons: Traffic can be horrendous, the climate would take some getting used to as it is often hot and humid, during the typhoon season you may encounter flooding in some areas, a lot of bureaucrats or corrupt officials in government, you need to &quot;follow-up&quot; a lot when you need to get something done - which can be frustrating at times, and if you don&#039;t have a car (which is probably unlikely) using the public transportation system is a nightmare.  By the way, driving in downtown Manila is not for the faint of heart.  <br /><br />I&#039;d like to expound on some of my earlier remarks.  Here are some examples of what I mean by the cost of living in Manila is lower: for the same amount of money you&#039;ll definitely get a bigger and better house in Manila as compared to Palo Alto, if you need to hire housemaids or nannies you&#039;ll just be paying $100-$150 per maid per month, if you&#039;ll be paying for your driver he&#039;ll cost just a bit more - around $160-$190 per month, a full body massage can be had for slightly less than $10 per hour, haircuts are slightly above $4 with a good barber and around $11 for having your hair cut at a salon (which typically includes shampooing and blow drying your hair).<br /><br />If you have the opportunity to meet the right people in Manila it can be extremely entertaining and fun to live in Manila. factored, why does this procedure work to find the factorization?&quot; Since I am taking the existence of a factorization as a given, let&#039;s start from the answer and see what that tells us.<br /><br />Suppose you have a polynomial that can be factored as:<br /><br /><img src=\"http://d3tf4rqyhjdxvy.cloudfront.net/main-707cb1d0695c9a99.png\" width=\"161\" height=\"21\" class=\"math\" title=\"(A x + B)(C x + D)\" alt=\"(A x + B)(C x + D)\" /><br /><br />Let&#039;s multiply this out, and keep the values in terms of these original four coefficients. That way, when we run the &quot;splitting the middle term&quot; algorithm, we can see what is really happening.<br /><br />So we start with:<br /><br /><img src=\"http://d3tf4rqyhjdxvy.cloudfront.net/main-3568cf46cb71bcdd.png\" width=\"242\" height=\"23\" class=\"math\" title=\"A C x^2 + (A D + B C)x + B D\" alt=\"A C x^2 + (A D + B C)x + B D\" /><br /><br />So in terms of the form <img src=\"http://d3tf4rqyhjdxvy.cloudfront.net/main-02c348a6b3d7a966.png\" width=\"106\" height=\"20\" class=\"math\" title=\"a x^2 + b x + c\" alt=\"a x^2 + b x + c\" /> we have:<br /><br /><img src=\"http://d3tf4rqyhjdxvy.cloudfront.net/main-b6f37133e28f61e6.png\" width=\"67\" height=\"14\" class=\"math\" title=\"a = A C\" alt=\"a = A C\" /><br /><img src=\"http://d3tf4rqyhjdxvy.cloudfront.net/main-6b17127446a64f62.png\" width=\"121\" height=\"16\" class=\"math\" title=\"b = A D + B C\" alt=\"b = A D + B C\" /><br /><img src=\"http://d3tf4rqyhjdxvy.cloudfront.net/main-090ac3be66ff2046.png\" width=\"66\" height=\"14\" class=\"math\" title=\"c = B D\" alt=\"c = B D\" /><br /><br />Now let&#039;s run the algorithm.<br /><br /><b>1) Find the master product</b><br /><br /><img src=\"http://d3tf4rqyhjdxvy.cloudfront.net/main-ebe2a79bc27a8471.png\" width=\"278\" height=\"19\" class=\"math\" title=\"\\text{mp} = a \\cdot c = A C \\cdot B D = A B C D\" alt=\"\\text{mp} = a \\cdot c = A C \\cdot B D = A B C D\" /><br /><br /><b>2) Find two factors of the master product that add up to </b><img src=\"http://d3tf4rqyhjdxvy.cloudfront.net/main-bc6a93f60bbd9730.png\" width=\"9\" height=\"14\" class=\"math\" title=\"b\" alt=\"b\" /><br /><br />In terms of the final answer, we see that the master product is <img src=\"http://d3tf4rqyhjdxvy.cloudfront.net/main-f3871951657fc3fa.png\" width=\"62\" height=\"14\" class=\"math\" title=\"A B C D\" alt=\"A B C D\" />. So it has as factors A, B, C, D, AB, AC, AD, BC, etc. It also has as factors the respective factors of A, B, C and D themselves, but it turns out we don&#039;t need those.<br /><br />The goal is to find some combination of factors of <img src=\"http://d3tf4rqyhjdxvy.cloudfront.net/main-f3871951657fc3fa.png\" width=\"62\" height=\"14\" class=\"math\" title=\"A B C D\" alt=\"A B C D\" /> that add up to <img src=\"http://d3tf4rqyhjdxvy.cloudfront.net/main-6b17127446a64f62.png\" width=\"121\" height=\"16\" class=\"math\" title=\"b = A D + B C\" alt=\"b = A D + B C\" />. Since we are already expressing all the values in terms of what we know will eventually be the answer, we essentially have X-ray vision into the numbers, and it is easy to see that the way to do this is to factor <img src=\"http://d3tf4rqyhjdxvy.cloudfront.net/main-f3871951657fc3fa.png\" width=\"62\" height=\"14\" class=\"math\" title=\"A B C D\" alt=\"A B C D\" /> into <img src=\"http://d3tf4rqyhjdxvy.cloudfront.net/main-6bdd8725edc5a3ab.png\" width=\"31\" height=\"14\" class=\"math\" title=\"A D\" alt=\"A D\" /> and <img src=\"http://d3tf4rqyhjdxvy.cloudfront.net/main-ca321b853cbe3aa6.png\" width=\"31\" height=\"14\" class=\"math\" title=\"B C\" alt=\"B C\" />. When you don&#039;t know the answer in advance, you are essentially finding <img src=\"http://d3tf4rqyhjdxvy.cloudfront.net/main-6bdd8725edc5a3ab.png\" width=\"31\" height=\"14\" class=\"math\" title=\"A D\" alt=\"A D\" /> and <img src=\"http://d3tf4rqyhjdxvy.cloudfront.net/main-ca321b853cbe3aa6.png\" width=\"31\" height=\"14\" class=\"math\" title=\"B C\" alt=\"B C\" /> by trial and error.<br /><br /><b>3) Split the center term (b) into the two chosen factors</b><br /><br />Notice that, with our X-ray vision, we see the original, unfactored starting polynomial like this:<br /><br /><img src=\"http://d3tf4rqyhjdxvy.cloudfront.net/main-3568cf46cb71bcdd.png\" width=\"242\" height=\"23\" class=\"math\" title=\"A C x^2 + (A D + B C)x + B D\" alt=\"A C x^2 + (A D + B C)x + B D\" /><br /><br />Since we found in step 2 that the two factors we want to use are <img src=\"http://d3tf4rqyhjdxvy.cloudfront.net/main-6bdd8725edc5a3ab.png\" width=\"31\" height=\"14\" class=\"math\" title=\"A D\" alt=\"A D\" /> and <img src=\"http://d3tf4rqyhjdxvy.cloudfront.net/main-ca321b853cbe3aa6.png\" width=\"31\" height=\"14\" class=\"math\" title=\"B C\" alt=\"B C\" />, step 3 now consists of rewriting the polynomial like this:<br /><br /><img src=\"http://d3tf4rqyhjdxvy.cloudfront.net/main-f637e5b181290821.png\" width=\"238\" height=\"20\" class=\"math\" title=\"A C x^2 + A D x + B C x + B D\" alt=\"A C x^2 + A D x + B C x + B D\" /><br /><br />In X-ray vision, this step looks ridiculously obvious now!<br /><br /><b>4) Group the terms into pairs and extract common factors</b><br /><br />Well, let&#039;s see. The first pair is <img src=\"http://d3tf4rqyhjdxvy.cloudfront.net/main-1caddcf5be192e74.png\" width=\"115\" height=\"20\" class=\"math\" title=\"A C x^2 + A D x\" alt=\"A C x^2 + A D x\" />.<br /><br />With X-ray vision, we see that clearly <img src=\"http://d3tf4rqyhjdxvy.cloudfront.net/main-55369620c787d8aa.png\" width=\"26\" height=\"14\" class=\"math\" title=\"A x\" alt=\"A x\" /> is a factor of both terms, so we&#039;ll write it as <img src=\"http://d3tf4rqyhjdxvy.cloudfront.net/main-caab3fef90bf2e6d.png\" width=\"107\" height=\"21\" class=\"math\" title=\"A x (C x + D)\" alt=\"A x (C x + D)\" />. Without X-ray vision, you would come to the same conclusion, but you&#039;d have to guess and check to find out what A is.<br /><br />The other pair is <img src=\"http://d3tf4rqyhjdxvy.cloudfront.net/main-865887b87635649c.png\" width=\"98\" height=\"16\" class=\"math\" title=\"B C x + B D\" alt=\"B C x + B D\" />, which now becomes <img src=\"http://d3tf4rqyhjdxvy.cloudfront.net/main-0d3aa5aded538fec.png\" width=\"97\" height=\"21\" class=\"math\" title=\"B(C x + D)\" alt=\"B(C x + D)\" />.<br /><br /><b>5) Factor out the common binomial</b><br /><br />Now we have <img src=\"http://d3tf4rqyhjdxvy.cloudfront.net/main-e5465c79492a499f.png\" width=\"229\" height=\"21\" class=\"math\" title=\"A x (C x + D) + B(C x + D)\" alt=\"A x (C x + D) + B(C x + D)\" />. It was inevitable that there would be a common binomial, because this is the form we originally started in before we expanded it. Well, we started very close to this; just undo the first step of the expansion and you get:<br /><br /><img src=\"http://d3tf4rqyhjdxvy.cloudfront.net/main-707cb1d0695c9a99.png\" width=\"161\" height=\"21\" class=\"math\" title=\"(A x + B)(C x + D)\" alt=\"(A x + B)(C x + D)\" /><br /><br />So in the end, the reason the procedure works is because, <b>if a polynomial can be factored</b>, the values <b>a, b </b>and<b> c </b>cannot have just any values. They have to have certain values that depend on the answer. The procedure takes advantage of the relationships between <b>a, b, c</b> and <b>A, B, C, D</b> to find one set from the other set. You are essentially solving the following system of equations for <b>A, B, C, D</b> using <b>a, b, c</b> as givens:<br /><br />(1) <img src=\"http://d3tf4rqyhjdxvy.cloudfront.net/main-b6f37133e28f61e6.png\" width=\"67\" height=\"14\" class=\"math\" title=\"a = A C\" alt=\"a = A C\" /><br />(2) <img src=\"http://d3tf4rqyhjdxvy.cloudfront.net/main-6b17127446a64f62.png\" width=\"121\" height=\"16\" class=\"math\" title=\"b = A D + B C\" alt=\"b = A D + B C\" /><br />(3) <img src=\"http://d3tf4rqyhjdxvy.cloudfront.net/main-090ac3be66ff2046.png\" width=\"66\" height=\"14\" class=\"math\" title=\"c = B D\" alt=\"c = B D\" /><br /><br />At first, it seems like there are four unknowns (<b>A, B, C, D</b>) and only three equations. However, there are really only three independent unknowns because there are multiple values of <b>A, B, C, D</b> that will produce the same values of <b>a, b, c</b>. Showing that <b>A, B, C, D</b> are not all independent is left as an exercise for the reader.", "css": "", "js": "W2.addComponentMetadata({parents: {\"zbMLfFt\": \"E732usZ\", \"r7HE2Dg\": \"zbMLfFt\",
